Parsing [sent. 3318 len. 12]: SWITZERLAND , THE CRADLE OF LIBERTY Interlaken , Switzerland , 1891 .
Parsing [sent. 3319 len. 13]: It is a good many years since I was in Switzerland last .
Parsing [sent. 3320 len. 14]: In that remote time there was only one ladder railway in the country .
Parsing [sent. 3321 len. 8]: That state of things is all changed .
Parsing [sent. 3322 len. 39]: There is n't a mountain in Switzerland now that has n't a ladder railroad or two up its back like suspenders ; indeed , some mountains are latticed with them , and two years hence all will be .
Parsing [sent. 3323 len. 37]: In that day the peasant of the high altitudes will have to carry a lantern when he goes visiting in the night to keep from stumbling over railroads that have been built since his last round .
Parsing [sent. 3324 len. 32]: And also in that day , if there shall remain a high-altitude peasant whose potato-patch has n't a railroad through it , it would make him as conspicuous as William Tell .
Parsing [sent. 3325 len. 13]: However , there are only two best ways to travel through Switzerland .
Parsing [sent. 3326 len. 6]: The first best is afloat .
Parsing [sent. 3327 len. 9]: The second best is by open two-horse carriage .
Parsing [sent. 3328 len. 47]: One can come from Lucerne to Interlaken over the Brunig by ladder railroad in an hour or so now , but you can glide smoothly in a carriage in ten , and have two hours for luncheon at noon -- for luncheon , not for rest .
Parsing [sent. 3329 len. 9]: There is no fatigue connected with the trip .
Parsing [sent. 3330 len. 37]: One arrives fresh in spirit and in person in the evening -- no fret in his heart , no grime on his face , no grit in his hair , not a cinder in his eye .
Parsing [sent. 3331 len. 47]: This is the right condition of mind and body , the right and due preparation for the solemn event which closed the day -- stepping with metaphorically uncovered head into the presence of the most impressive mountain mass that the globe can show -- the Jungfrau .
Parsing [sent. 3332 len. 26]: The stranger 's first feeling , when suddenly confronted by that towering and awful apparition wrapped in its shroud of snow , is breath-taking astonishment .
Parsing [sent. 3333 len. 15]: It is as if heaven 's gates had swung open and exposed the throne .
Parsing [sent. 3334 len. 9]: It is peaceful here and pleasant at Interlaken .
Parsing [sent. 3335 len. 12]: Nothing going on -- at least nothing but brilliant life-giving sunshine .
Parsing [sent. 3336 len. 8]: There are floods and floods of that .
Parsing [sent. 3337 len. 33]: One may properly speak of it as `` going on , '' for it is full of the suggestion of activity ; the light pours down with energy , with visible enthusiasm .
Parsing [sent. 3338 len. 15]: This is a good atmosphere to be in , morally as well as physically .
Parsing [sent. 3339 len. 60]: After trying the political atmosphere of the neighboring monarchies , it is healing and refreshing to breathe air that has known no taint of slavery for six hundred years , and to come among a people whose political history is great and fine , and worthy to be taught in all schools and studied by all races and peoples .
Parsing [sent. 3340 len. 45]: For the struggle here throughout the centuries has not been in the interest of any private family , or any church , but in the interest of the whole body of the nation , and for shelter and protection of all forms of belief .

Parsing [sent. 4167 len. 29]: A SIMPLIFIED ALPHABET -LRB- This article , written during the autumn of 1899 , was about the last writing done by Mark Twain on any impersonal subject . -RRB-
Parsing [sent. 4168 len. 35]: I have had a kindly feeling , a friendly feeling , a cousinly feeling toward Simplified Spelling , from the beginning of the movement three years ago , but nothing more inflamed than that .
Parsing [sent. 4169 len. 43]: It seemed to me to merely propose to substitute one inadequacy for another ; a sort of patching and plugging poor old dental relics with cement and gold and porcelain paste ; what was really needed was a new set of teeth .
Parsing [sent. 4170 len. 9]: That is to say , a new ALPHABET .
Parsing [sent. 4171 len. 11]: The heart of our trouble is with our foolish alphabet .
Parsing [sent. 4172 len. 14]: It does n't know how to spell , and ca n't be taught .
Parsing [sent. 4173 len. 14]: In this it is like all other alphabets except one -- the phonographic .
Parsing [sent. 4174 len. 10]: This is the only competent alphabet in the world .
Parsing [sent. 4175 len. 12]: It can spell and correctly pronounce any word in our language .
Parsing [sent. 4176 len. 21]: That admirable alphabet , that brilliant alphabet , that inspired alphabet , can be learned in an hour or two .
Parsing [sent. 4177 len. 23]: In a week the student can learn to write it with some little facility , and to read it with considerable ease .
Parsing [sent. 4178 len. 35]: I know , for I saw it tried in a public school in Nevada forty-five years ago , and was so impressed by the incident that it has remained in my memory ever since .
Parsing [sent. 4179 len. 18]: I wish we could adopt it in place of our present written -LRB- and printed -RRB- character .
Parsing [sent. 4180 len. 38]: I mean SIMPLY the alphabet ; simply the consonants and the vowels -- I do n't mean any REDUCTIONS or abbreviations of them , such as the shorthand writer uses in order to get compression and speed .
Parsing [sent. 4181 len. 9]: No , I would SPELL EVERY WORD OUT .
Parsing [sent. 4182 len. 16]: I will insert the alphabet here as I find it in Burnz 's PHONIC SHORTHAND .
Parsing [sent. 4183 len. 16]: -LRB- Figure 1 -RRB- It is arranged on the basis of Isaac Pitman 's PHONOGRAPHY .
Parsing [sent. 4184 len. 11]: Isaac Pitman was the originator and father of scientific phonography .
Parsing [sent. 4185 len. 7]: It is used throughout the globe .
Parsing [sent. 4186 len. 6]: It was a memorable invention .
Parsing [sent. 4187 len. 8]: He made it public seventy-three years ago .
Parsing [sent. 4188 len. 22]: The firm of Isaac Pitman & Sons , New York , still exists , and they continue the master 's work .
Parsing [sent. 4189 len. 5]: What should we gain ?
Parsing [sent. 4190 len. 24]: First of all , we could spell DEFINITELY -- and correctly -- any word you please , just by the SOUND of it .
Parsing [sent. 4191 len. 10]: We ca n't do that with our present alphabet .
Parsing [sent. 4192 len. 11]: For instance , take a simple , every-day word PHTHISIS .
Parsing [sent. 4193 len. 27]: If we tried to spell it by the sound of it , we should make it TYSIS , and be laughed at by every educated person .
Parsing [sent. 4194 len. 12]: Secondly , we should gain in REDUCTION OF LABOR in writing .
Parsing [sent. 4195 len. 21]: Simplified Spelling makes valuable reductions in the case of several hundred words , but the new spelling must be LEARNED .
Parsing [sent. 4196 len. 18]: You ca n't spell them by the sound ; you must get them out of the book .
Parsing [sent. 4197 len. 37]: But even if we knew the simplified form for every word in the language , the phonographic alphabet would still beat the Simplified Speller `` hands down '' in the important matter of economy of labor .
Parsing [sent. 4198 len. 13]: I will illustrate : PRESENT FORM : through , laugh , highland .
Parsing [sent. 4199 len. 9]: SIMPLIFIED FORM : thru , laff , hyland .
Parsing [sent. 4200 len. 23]: PHONOGRAPHIC FORM : -LRB- Figure 2 -RRB- To write the word `` through , '' the pen has to make twenty-one strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4201 len. 20]: To write the word `` thru , '' then pen has to make twelve strokes -- a good saving .
Parsing [sent. 4202 len. 19]: To write that same word with the phonographic alphabet , the pen has to make only THREE strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4203 len. 16]: To write the word `` laugh , '' the pen has to make FOURTEEN strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4204 len. 25]: To write `` laff , '' the pen has to make the SAME NUMBER of strokes -- no labor is saved to the penman .
Parsing [sent. 4205 len. 19]: To write the same word with the phonographic alphabet , the pen has to make only THREE strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4206 len. 16]: To write the word `` highland , '' the pen has to make twenty-two strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4207 len. 14]: To write `` hyland , '' the pen has to make eighteen strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4208 len. 18]: To write that word with the phonographic alphabet , the pen has to make only FIVE strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4209 len. 21]: -LRB- Figure 3 -RRB- To write the words `` phonographic alphabet , '' the pen has to make fifty-three strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4210 len. 15]: To write `` fonografic alfabet , '' the pen has to make fifty strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4211 len. 11]: To the penman , the saving in labor is insignificant .
Parsing [sent. 4212 len. 22]: To write that word -LRB- with vowels -RRB- with the phonographic alphabet , the pen has to make only SEVENTEEN strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4213 len. 8]: Without the vowels , only THIRTEEN strokes .
Parsing [sent. 4214 len. 13]: -LRB- Figure 4 -RRB- The vowels are hardly necessary , this time .
Parsing [sent. 4215 len. 37]: We make five pen-strokes in writing an m. Thus : -LRB- Figure 5 -RRB- a stroke down ; a stroke up ; a second stroke down ; a second stroke up ; a final stroke down .
Parsing [sent. 4216 len. 4]: Total , five .
Parsing [sent. 4217 len. 45]: The phonographic alphabet accomplishes the m with a single stroke -- a curve , like a parenthesis that has come home drunk and has fallen face down right at the front door where everybody that goes along will see him and say , Alas !
Parsing [sent. 4218 len. 46]: When our written m is not the end of a word , but is otherwise located , it has to be connected with the next letter , and that requires another pen-stroke , making six in all , before you get rid of that m .
Parsing [sent. 4219 len. 12]: But never mind about the connecting strokes -- let them go .
Parsing [sent. 4220 len. 24]: Without counting them , the twenty-six letters of our alphabet consumed about eighty pen-strokes for their construction -- about three pen-strokes per letter .
Parsing [sent. 4221 len. 12]: It is THREE TIMES THE NUMBER required by the phonographic alphabet .
Parsing [sent. 4222 len. 9]: It requires but ONE stroke for each letter .

Parsing [sent. 4921 len. 28]: IV Conjectures The historians `` suppose '' that Shakespeare attended the Free School in Stratford from the time he was seven years old till he was thirteen .
Parsing [sent. 4922 len. 15]: There is no EVIDENCE in existence that he ever went to school at all .
Parsing [sent. 4923 len. 24]: The historians `` infer '' that he got his Latin in that school -- the school which they `` suppose '' he attended .

Parsing [sent. 4925 len. 19]: But there is no evidence that he ever entered or returned from the school they suppose he attended .
Parsing [sent. 4926 len. 35]: They `` suppose '' he assisted his father in the butchering business ; and that , being only a boy , he did n't have to do full-grown butchering , but only slaughtering calves .
Parsing [sent. 4927 len. 16]: Also , that whenever he killed a calf he made a high-flown speech over it .

Parsing [sent. 4930 len. 2]: Curious .

Parsing [sent. 4934 len. 31]: For experience is an author 's most valuable asset ; experience is the thing that puts the muscle and the breath and the warm blood into the book he writes .

Parsing [sent. 4950 len. 5]: It is sorely embarrassing .
Parsing [sent. 4951 len. 62]: If he began to slaughter calves , and poach deer , and rollick around , and learn English , at the earliest likely moment -- say at thirteen , when he was supposably wretched from that school where he was supposably storing up Latin for future literary use -- he had his youthful hands full , and much more than full .
Parsing [sent. 4952 len. 25]: He must have had to put aside his Warwickshire dialect , which would n't be understood in London , and study English very hard .
Parsing [sent. 4953 len. 55]: Very hard indeed ; incredibly hard , almost , if the result of that labor was to be the smooth and rounded and flexible and letter-perfect English of the `` Venus and Adonis '' in the space of ten years ; and at the same time learn great and fine and unsurpassable literary FORM .
Parsing [sent. 4954 len. 138]: However , it is `` conjectured '' that he accomplished all this and more , much more : learned law and its intricacies ; and the complex procedure of the law-courts ; and all about soldiering , and sailoring , and the manners and customs and ways of royal courts and aristocratic society ; and likewise accumulated in his one head every kind of knowledge the learned then possessed , and every kind of humble knowledge possessed by the lowly and the ignorant ; and added thereto a wider and more intimate knowledge of the world 's great literatures , ancient and modern , than was possessed by any other man of his time -- for he was going to make brilliant and easy and admiration-compelling use of these splendid treasures the moment he got to London .

Parsing [sent. 4958 len. 94]: It is surmised by the biographers that the young Shakespeare got his vast knowledge of the law and his familiar and accurate acquaintance with the manners and customs and shop-talk of lawyers through being for a time the CLERK OF A STRATFORD COURT ; just as a bright lad like me , reared in a village on the banks of the Mississippi , might become perfect in knowledge of the Bering Strait whale-fishery and the shop-talk of the veteran exercises of that adventure-bristling trade through catching catfish with a `` trot-line '' Sundays .
Parsing [sent. 4959 len. 30]: But the surmise is damaged by the fact that there is no evidence -- and not even tradition -- that the young Shakespeare was ever clerk of a law-court .

Parsing [sent. 5151 len. 56]: VIII Shakespeare as a Lawyer -LRB- 1 -RRB- The Plays and Poems of Shakespeare supply ample evidence that their author not only had a very extensive and accurate knowledge of law , but that he was well acquainted with the manners and customs of members of the Inns of Court and with legal life generally .
Parsing [sent. 5152 len. 50]: `` While novelists and dramatists are constantly making mistakes as to the laws of marriage , of wills , of inheritance , to Shakespeare 's law , lavishly as he expounds it , there can neither be demurrer , nor bill of exceptions , nor writ of error . ''
Parsing [sent. 5153 len. 36]: Such was the testimony borne by one of the most distinguished lawyers of the nineteenth century who was raised to the high office of Lord Chief Justice in 1850 , and subsequently became Lord Chancellor .
Parsing [sent. 5154 len. 52]: Its weight will , doubtless , be more appreciated by lawyers than by laymen , for only lawyers know how impossible it is for those who have not served an apprenticeship to the law to avoid displaying their ignorance if they venture to employ legal terms and to discuss legal doctrines .

Parsing [sent. 5165 len. 11]: And what does the same high authority say about Shakespeare ?

Parsing [sent. 5171 len. 61]: Another lawyer and well-known Shakespearean , Richard Grant White , says : `` No dramatist of the time , not even Beaumont , who was the younger son of a judge of the Common Pleas , and who after studying in the Inns of Court abandoned law for the drama , used legal phrases with Shakespeare 's readiness and exactness .

Parsing [sent. 5173 len. 48]: The phrases peculiar to other occupations serve him on rare occasions by way of description , comparison , or illustration , generally when something in the scene suggests them , but legal phrases flow from his pen as part of his vocabulary and parcel of his thought .
Parsing [sent. 5174 len. 70]: Take the word ` purchase ' for instance , which , in ordinary use , means to acquire by giving value , but applies in law to all legal modes of obtaining property except by inheritance or descent , and in this peculiar sense the word occurs five times in Shakespeare 's thirty-four plays , and only in one single instance in the fifty-four plays of Beaumont and Fletcher .
Parsing [sent. 5175 len. 22]: It has been suggested that it was in attendance upon the courts in London that he picked up his legal vocabulary .
Parsing [sent. 5176 len. 124]: But this supposition not only fails to account for Shakespeare 's peculiar freedom and exactness in the use of that phraseology , it does not even place him in the way of learning those terms his use of which is most remarkable , which are not such as he would have heard at ordinary proceedings at NISI PRIUS , but such as refer to the tenure or transfer of real property , ` fine and recovery , ' ` statutes merchant , ' ` purchase , ' ` indenture , ' ` tenure , ' ` double voucher , ' ` fee simple , ' ` fee farm , ' ` remainder , ' ` reversion , ' ` forfeiture , ' etc. .
